    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="7e147576cda743608ccb4a1cb28c9b99">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '19f25ff1-f2e9-42b8-bb7a-4c191ce0c69a'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d258196192" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Due to the relatively high capacity and lower cost, transition metal sulfides (TMS) as anode show promising potential in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). Herein, a binary metal sulfide hybrid consisting of carbon encapsulated CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S nanocages (CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC) is constructed. The interlocked hetero-architecture filled with conductive carbon accelerates the Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transfer, thus leading to improved electrochemical kinetics. Also the protective carbon layer can provide better volume accommondation upon charging/discharging. As a result, the battery with CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC as anode displays a high capacity of 435.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (≈3.4&nbsp;C). Under a higher rate of 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (≈17&nbsp;C), a capacity of as high as 347.2&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> is still remained after long 2300 cycles. The capacity decay per cycle is only 0.017%. The battery also exhibits a better temperature tolerance at 50 and −5&nbsp;°C. A low internal impedance analyzed by X-ray diffraction patterns and galvanostatic intermittent titration technique, narrow band gap, and high density of states obtained by first-principle calculations of the binary sulfides, ensure the rapid Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transport. The long-cycling-life SIB using binary metal sulfide hybrid nanocages as anode shows promising applications in versatile electronic devices.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302706-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302706-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>The emerging electronic devices and electric vehicles necessitate the needs in large energy-density energy storage such as lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0001_R_d258195871e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Renew. Sust. Energ. Rev.">1</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0002_R_d258195871e410" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy.">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Scientists are seeking alternatives for lithium due to the high cost and safety issues. Sodium-ion battery is considered as a promising secondary battery due to the high capacity, abundance of sodium, low cost, and environmental friendliness.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0003_R_d258195871e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>-<a href="#smll202302706-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0005_R_d258195871e415" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Adv. Energy Mater.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Currently, the commercialization of SIB is partially restricted by desired anode materials, which can provide better accommodation for volumetric expansion upon large Na<sup>+</sup> insertion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0006_R_d258195871e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the basis of multi-electron reaction, transition metal sulfides exhibit high theoretical capacity as anode materials in SIB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0007_R_d258195871e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nature">7</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0008_R_d258195871e428" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Energy Chem.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Further, interfacial engineering allows the integration of metal sulfide into a specific configuration with increased electronic conductivity, which typically presents better cycling stability and electrochemical activity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0009_R_d258195871e433" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Acc. Chem. Res.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A micro-/nano-binary metal sulfide (CoSn)S heterostructure encapsulated in carbon shell showed a reversible capacity of 368&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> along with a high mechanical strength.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0010_R_d258195871e443" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to further improve the cyclability and rate-performance,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0011_R_d258195871e448" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Adv. Mater.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Je et&nbsp;al. synthesized a mesoporous CuS/FeS<sub>2</sub>@NC nanocube that showed enhanced sodium storage with excellent electrolyte impregnation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0012_R_d258195871e456" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Small">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A bimetallic sulfide FeCoS<sub>4</sub>@carbon nanotube was developed, which prevented the growth of large Na<sub>2</sub>S clusters.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0013_R_d258195871e466" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Chem. Eng. J.">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A covalent coupling-stabilized TMS/carbon nanotube heterojunction ZnS/CNT was designed, which presented pseudocapacitive behavior and reversible electrochemical processes, leading to long-term stability and rate capability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0014_R_d258195871e471" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - ACS Nano">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A binary iron sulfide FeS<sub>2</sub>/Fe<sub>7</sub>S<sub>8</sub>-reduced graphene oxide (rGO) composite synthesized via a solvothermal method also exhibited both high Li- and Na-storage capacities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0015_R_d258195871e483" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Appl. Mater. Interfaces">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A hybrid that is composed of two metal sulfides displays promising potential in reliable Na-ion batteries with long cycling life.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0016_R_d258195871e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Adv. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0017_R_d258195871e488" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - ACS Nano">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The typical 2D binary metal sulfides showed competitive battery performance in SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0018_R_d258195871e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - ACS Nano">18</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0019_R_d258195871e494" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - ACS Nano">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>In this article, we designed a binary metal sulfide hybrid called CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC that conatains CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S composite encapsulated in a nitrogen-doped carbon shell. The obtained hybrid configuration as anode showed beneficial features in high-performance SIBs: i) the bimetallic sulfides provide large theoretical capacity for Na<sup>+</sup> ions; ii) the binary nano-cage architecture faciliates the Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transport along the carbon covered nano-channels, which redcues the internal resistance and leads to a better cycling stability even under varying temperatures; iii) the spherical cage structure provides sufficient space to accommodate the volume expansion/shrinkage during charging/discharging. The battery with the synthesized CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC hybrid as anode displayed an excellent cycling life up to 2300 cycles under a high current desity of 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302706-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302706-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CuS precursor was prepared by sulfidation of Co/Cu nitrate salts through a facile solvothermal method. A cage morphology with a diameter of about 1&nbsp;µm was observed by field emission scanning electron microscope (SEM) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202302706-fig-0001">1a</a>). The X–ray powder diffraction (XRD) pattern confirmed the crystal structures of both Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> and CuS (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information). During the successive calcination process, the Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub> was reduced to CoS and CuS was reduced to Cu<sub>2</sub>S by carbon (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202302706-fig-0002">2a</a>). The characteristic peaks are indexed to CoS with (100), (101), (102), (110), and (202) planes and Cu<sub>2</sub>S with (102), (111), (104), (200), (201) planes, indicating the coexistence of both CoS and Cu<sub>2</sub>S phases. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0001">1b</a> shows the spherical morphology of the formed CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC with rough surface. High-magnification transmission electron microscope (TEM) images reveal the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S particle was encapsulated by a thin carbon layer with a thickness of 20–30&nbsp;nm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0001">1c,d</a>). As shown in the high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) image (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0001">1e</a>), the lattice spacings of 0.327 and 0.377&nbsp;nm correspond to (102) and (101) planes of Cu<sub>2</sub>S while the spacing of 0.293&nbsp;nm correlates to the (100) plane of CoS. More importantly, the boundary of CoS and Cu<sub>2</sub>S is clearly observed, as marked by the dashed lines. The abundant phase boundaries generated by bimetallic sulfides provide fast Na<sup>+</sup> ion transfer channels which ensures high rate-performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0020_R_d258195871e581" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern also further concludes the polycrystalline structure of binary sulfides (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0001">1f</a>). The elemental mappings of the sample confirm the chemical composition of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S cage wrapped by N-doped carbon shell (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0001">1g</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3a–c</a>, Supporting Information). Based on the energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) results, the ratio of Cu and Co atoms is ≈1:3, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3c</a>, Supporting Information). As a reference, the crystal structure and morphology of CoS@C and Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302706-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/fd05519e-fdb3-4eb6-8a03-f30961158255/smll202302706-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302706-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>

                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM morphologies of a) Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CuS precursor and b) CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC. c) TEM and d,e) HRTEM morphologies and the corresponding f) SAED pattern and g) EDS elemental mappings of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302706-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/86df805e-e49a-4c3a-98e9-73de08f852a2/smll202302706-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/86df805e-e49a-4c3a-98e9-73de08f852a2/smll202302706-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/6a9acb0f-0a18-4d24-a11a-861ddc03324e/smll202302706-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/86df805e-e49a-4c3a-98e9-73de08f852a2/smll202302706-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302706-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern of the as-obtained CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC. High-resolution XPS spectra of b) Co 2p, c) Cu 2p, d) S 2p, e) C 1 s, and f) N 1s of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>The as-received CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC was analyzed by using thermogravimetric analysis (TGA) in air from room temperature to 1000 °C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4a</a>, Supporting Information). The slight mass loss below 230&nbsp;°C is ascribed to the evaporation of adsorbed water. The sharp weight gain at 400–570&nbsp;°C corresponds to the formation of CuO∙CuSO<sub>4</sub> and CuSO<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0021_R_d258195871e670" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Under higher temperature between 720 and 900°C, the CuO·CuSO<sub>4</sub> begins to decompose to CuO and Cu<sub>2</sub>O, resulting in a mass loss. Then CoS is oxidized into Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0010_R_d258195871e685" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Colloid Interface Sci.">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Based on the final 75.69&nbsp;wt% of Cu<sub>2</sub>O and Co<sub>3</sub>O<sub>4</sub>, the weight ratio of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S in CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC is ≈84.7% (68.61&nbsp;wt% for CoS and 16.09&nbsp;wt% for Cu<sub>2</sub>S), and the corresponding carbon ratio is 15.3%. Two feature peaks of D band at 1366.7&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> and G band at 1583.15&nbsp;cm<sup>−1</sup> in the Raman spectrum demonstrate the existence of carbon layer (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4b</a>, Supporting Information), which contributes to a high conductivity.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0022_R_d258195871e711" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Sci. Bull">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET) measurement shows the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC has a surface area of 7.27&nbsp;m<sup>2</sup>&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information) and tiny mesoporous pores ranging 1–3&nbsp;nm (insert), which provide large contact interface with electrolyte, thus leading to a fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</p>
         
         <p>X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) survey displays that the as-obtained CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC contains Co, Cu, S, C, N, and O (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), which is in agreement with the EDS data in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0001">1g</a>. The high-resolution Co 2p spectrum shows that peaks centered at 780.9 and 797.8&nbsp;eV are associated with Co 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Co 2p<sub>1/2</sub> orbitals, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0002">2b</a>). The peak at 786.5&nbsp;eV is ascribed to the Co<span class="icomoon"></span>O bond from surface oxidation of CoS. The Cu 2p spectrum can be deconvoluted into two pairs of peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0002">2c</a>). The peaks at 932.1/934.3 and 952.0/954.4&nbsp;eV correspond to Cu 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and Cu 2p<sub>1/2</sub> for Cu<sup>+</sup>, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0023_R_d258195871e757" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In the S 2p spectrum (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0002">2d</a>), peaks at 161.6 and 162.6&nbsp;eV are from S 2p<sub>3/2</sub> and 2p<sub>1/2</sub> of the Co<span class="icomoon"></span>S bond in CoS. Other peaks at ≈163.7 and 165.0&nbsp;eV are correlated with the C<span class="icomoon"></span>S<span class="icomoon"></span>C bond. In addition, the weak peak at 167.8&nbsp;eV corresponds to sulfite (SO<sub>3</sub><sup>2−</sup>), which was formed due to the surface oxidation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0024_R_d258195871e773" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Chem. Eng. J.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The C 1s spectrum shows three peaks at 284.6, 285.7, and 288.8&nbsp;eV, corresponding to sp<sup>2</sup>/sp<sup>3</sup> hybridized carbon C<span class="icomoon"></span>C/CC, C<span class="icomoon"></span>N and O<span class="icomoon"></span>CO, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0002">2e</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0025_R_d258195871e786" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Adv. Energy Mater.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The N 1s curve is divided into three different peaks of pyridinic N at 397.9, pyrrolic N at 400.0, and graphitic N at 402.5&nbsp;eV (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0002">2f</a>).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0026_R_d258195871e794" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - J. Mater. Chem. A">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC under a potential range of 0.2–3.0&nbsp;V are shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202302706-fig-0003">3a</a>. The first-cycle peak at 0.62&nbsp;V is due to the formation of solid electrolyte interface (SEI) on the electrode. The multi-redox peaks indicate the combined intercalation and conversion reactions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0021_R_d258195871e810" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Chem. Eng. J.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Specifically the reduction peak at 0.91&nbsp;V corresponds to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into Cu<sub>2</sub>S to form Na<sub>x</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>S and the successive conversion reaction of intermediate phase Na<sub>x</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>S to Cu and Na<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0027_R_d258195871e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Matter.">27</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0028_R_d258195871e831" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peaks at 0.58 and 1.19&nbsp;V are associated with the conversion reactions of CoS. Other small peaks at 1.55 and 1.95&nbsp;V are ascribed to the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> ions. During the anodic scanning, the oxidation peaks at 1.56 and 1.72&nbsp;V are originated from reversible conversions from Cu/Na<sub>2</sub>S to Na<sub>x</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>S and from Co/Na<sub>2</sub>S to CoS. The peak at 2.12&nbsp;V represents the denaturation from Na<sub>x</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>S to Cu<sub>2</sub>S.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0027_R_d258195871e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Matter.">27</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0028_R_d258195871e854" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is seen that almost all redox peaks remain same location from the second to fifth cycles, indicating a good electrochemical stability. Further, the galvanostatic charge/discharge profiles also show redox plateaus agreement with the CV curves (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0003">3b</a>).</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302706-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e2ab5712-62b3-4d71-99b8-17f213833034/smll202302706-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e2ab5712-62b3-4d71-99b8-17f213833034/smll202302706-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a2d59b17-f59e-4517-8de8-1d2da609983a/smll202302706-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e2ab5712-62b3-4d71-99b8-17f213833034/smll202302706-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302706-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC as anode at a scanning rate of 0.1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Charge–discharge profiles of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC upon cycling at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. Cycling performance of the Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C, CoS@C, and CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC at c) 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>and d) 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Long-life cyclability of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC at 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. All cells were activated at 0.1 A g<sup>−1</sup> for 6 cycles.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>Half-cell battery performance using the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC as anode was also checked. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0003">3c</a>, it displays a good capacity retention of 455.9&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a negligible 0.031% decay per cycle and a high coulombic efficiency (CE) of 99.5% after 450 cycles. The small fluctuation during initial cycles was originated from the SEI formation, Na<sup>+</sup> ion depletion, and possibly irreversible side reactions. As a comparison, due to the structure collapse, the capacity of both CoS@C and Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C dropped rapidly. Under a higher current density of 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC exhibited a long-term capacity retention of 435.3&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a 0.009% capacity decay per cycle after 1000 cycles (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0003">3d</a>). In contrast, the battery with CoS@C and Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C showed poor cycling stability along with a CE of &lt;80%. More importantly, the battery with CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC showed excellent long-cycling life under an ultra-high charge/discharge rate of 10.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. As seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0003">3e</a>, the battery still showed a capacity ≈347.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 2300 cycles. The capacity decay for each cycle was only 0.017% and the CE was ≈100%. It should be noted that the slight capacity uptake at beginning was derived from the gradual penetration of electrolyte and the activation of materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0029_R_d258195871e941" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Nano Energy">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, the battery displayed reliable capacity upon cycling with high active material loadings (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information) and low rate (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The full cell battery paired with Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP) as cathode also showed a stable capacity at 303.6&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (vs anode active material) along with a CE of 97.8% after 50 cycles at 0.5&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9a,b</a>, Supporting Information). During the synthesis of CoS and Cu<sub>2</sub>S, the synergistic formation of interlocked heterojunction shortens the Na<sup>+</sup> ion transport distance. Compared with monometallic sulfides, the binary metal sulfides have higher electrical conductivity, in which the mixed valence and electronic synergy of metal ions increase the reaction kinetics, therefore receiving high rate-performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0030_R_d258195871e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Adv. Mater.">30</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0031_R_d258195871e973" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Energy Stor. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The battery performance comparison with Cu<sub>2</sub>S- and CoS-based materials as anode was illustrated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
         
         <p>Rate capability of the battery with different anode materials was investigated under varying current densities of 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1.0, 2.0, and 5.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202302706-fig-0004">4a</a>). It was found that high capacities of 521.5, 505.2, 480.4, 459.9, 437.1, and 396.0&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> and high CE with CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC were achieved, which were much higher than the capacities with Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C and CoS@C. The battery with CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC as anode also displayed good temperature tolerance. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0004">4b,a</a> capacity of 472.3&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> with a high CE was remained under 50&nbsp;°C after 170 cycles at 1.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>. At a low temperature of 5&nbsp;°C, the capacity of 375.2&nbsp;mA&nbsp;h&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> along with a CE of &gt;99.9% was obtained after 900 cycles at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0004">4c</a>). It is regarded that the binary sulfides architecture with multi-phase interfaces that allow rapid ion/electron transport contributed to the improved electrochemical performance under broad temperatures.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0032_R_d258195871e1019" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Appl. Catal. B">32</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Charge transfer resistance (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) of the battery was analyzed by using the Nyquist plot from electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0004">4d</a>). It is seen that the transfer resistance <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> with CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC is ≈57.06&nbsp;Ω, which is smaller than the CoS@C (141.16&nbsp;Ω) and Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C (120.7&nbsp;Ω). After the battery was cycled 100 times at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup>, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> value of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC was decreased to ≈10.06&nbsp;Ω, which is much lower than CoS@C and Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0004">4e</a>). The greatly reduced charge transfer resistance of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC is benefited from the enhanced electronic conductivity of the designed hybrid structure. A cage-like configuration of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC after 500 cycles at 2.0&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> was still remained (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0004">4f</a>). It is believed that the carbon coating and the robust binary structure prevent the active material loss during charging/discharging.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0033_R_d258195871e1061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Chem. Eng. J.">33</a>, <a href="#smll202302706-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0034_R_d258195871e1061" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Chem. Eng. J.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302706-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9a382207-0952-4a25-bf7a-3e669556a48e/smll202302706-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9a382207-0952-4a25-bf7a-3e669556a48e/smll202302706-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e6f20f9d-5be4-4485-8ff2-2f3098d356a6/smll202302706-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9a382207-0952-4a25-bf7a-3e669556a48e/smll202302706-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302706-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Rate-performance of the Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C, CoS@C, and CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC as anode. The CE of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC is shown in the right. b) Cycling performance of the battery with the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC as anode under b) high-temperature of 50&nbsp;°C at 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and c) low-temperature of −5&nbsp;°C at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. EIS spectra of the CoS@C, Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C, and CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC with d) fresh cells and e) cells after 100 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. f) SEM morphology of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC after 500 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         <p>In order to further discover the reaction mechanism of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC hybrid, the capacity contribution (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#smll202302706-fig-0005">5a</a>) and ex situ XRD pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0005">5b</a>) at different discharge/charge states were checked. Two peaks at 35° and ≈62° are indexed to the CoS (101) and (103) planes. Another peak at 46° corresponds to the Cu<sub>2</sub>S (201) plane. As the potential dropped to 2.0&nbsp;V upon discharge, these three peaks became broad, indicating the insertion of Na<sup>+</sup> into the Cu<sub>2</sub>S and CoS to form Na<sub>x</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>S and Na<sub>x</sub>CoS. As the potential proceeded to 1.0&nbsp;V, diffraction peaks of CoS disappeared, while the peak of Cu<sub>2</sub>S (113) at ≈39° was found. When the battery was discharged to 0.2&nbsp;V and charged to 1.0&nbsp;V, a new peak that is assigned with Na<sub>2</sub>S (220) plane (JCPDS No. 03–0933) appeared (marked with a blue diamond), which indicates the conversion reactions from Na<sub>x</sub>Cu<sub>2</sub>S to Cu and Na<sub>x</sub>CoS to Co. Due to the random distribution and low crystallinity, the Cu and Co were almost non-detectable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0035_R_d258195871e1146" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Adv. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The strong Cu peaks are mainly from the copper current collector. With the charge potential increased to 3.0&nbsp;V, the Na<sub>2</sub>S peak disappeared due to the fully de-sodiation. Instead, the peaks of Cu<sub>2</sub>S (102) and CoS (200) were recovered. These results confirm the reversible complex reaction mechanism that combines the intercalation and conversion. The reactions are presented as following equations:</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="smll202302706-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dd95ebda-8f67-4324-9644-4bd1604da52c/smll202302706-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/dd95ebda-8f67-4324-9644-4bd1604da52c/smll202302706-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/62b8a365-2ce7-4879-9250-84b5ca12911a/smll202302706-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dd95ebda-8f67-4324-9644-4bd1604da52c/smll202302706-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=smll202302706-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fsmll.202302706" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The battery capacity versus potential at 200th cycle under 1.0 A g<sup>−1</sup>. b) Ex situ XRD patterns of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC at varying cut-off voltages during the initial discharge/charge. In situ reaction impedances of the CoS@C, Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C, and CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC during c) discharge and d) charge by using GITT at a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Band gap structure and f) density of states (DOS) of the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S hybrid.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>

                                       <mi>Na</mi>
                                       <mo>+</mo>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <mrow></mrow>
                                    <mfenced close=")" open="(" separators="">
                                       <mrow>
                                          <mn>2</mn>
                                          <mo>−</mo>
                                          <mi mathvariant="normal">x</mi>
                                       </mrow>
                                    </mfenced>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">e</mi>
                                       <mo>−</mo>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mo>→</mo>
                                    <mrow>

                                    </mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi>
                                       <mn>2</mn>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mtd>
                           </mtr>
                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}{\rm{Na}}_{\rm{x}}{\rm{CoS }} + {\rm{ }}\left( {{\rm{2 - x}}} \right){\rm{ Na}}^ + + {\rm{ }}\left( {{\rm{2 - x}}} \right){{\rm{e}}}^ - \to {\rm{Co + N}}{{\rm{a}}}_{\rm{2}}{\rm{S}}\end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
         </div>

         <div class="paragraph-element">Deintercalation (charge):

            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202302706-disp-0007"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->

                        <mtable displaystyle="true">

                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}{\rm{Na}}_{\rm{x}}{\rm{Cu}}_2{\rm{S}} \to {\rm{Cu}}_2{\rm{S }} + {\rm{ xNa}}^ + + {\rm{ xe}}^ - \end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(7)</span></div>
            <div class="inline-equation" id="smll202302706-disp-0008"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                  <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/e6125db9-14e5-4c0f-ab2d-f686a92fed86/smll202302706-math-0008.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:16136810:media:smll202302706:smll202302706-math-0008" display="block" location="graphic/smll202302706-math-0008.png">
                     <semantics>
                        <mtable displaystyle="true">
                           <mtr>
                              <mtd columnalign="right">
                                 <mrow>
                                    <msub>
                                       <mi>Na</mi>
                                       <mi>x</mi>
                                    </msub>
                                    <mi>CoS</mi>
                                    <mo>→</mo>
                                    <mi>CoS</mi>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>xNa</mi>
                                       <mo>+</mo>
                                    </msup>
                                    <mo linebreak="goodbreak">+</mo>
                                    <msup>
                                       <mi>xe</mi>
                                       <mo>−</mo>
                                    </msup>
                                 </mrow>
                              </mtd>
                           </mtr>
                        </mtable>
                        <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray}{\rm{Na}}_x{\rm{CoS}} \to {\rm{CoS }} + {\rm{ xNa}}^ + + {\rm{ xe}}^ - \end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                     </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(8)</span></div>
         </div>
         
         <p>CV profiles of the battery with CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC at varying scan rates from 0.1 to 1&nbsp;mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup> are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11a</a> (Supporting Information). The higher potential shift for oxidic peaks and lower potential shift for cathodic peaks demonstrated the electrochemical polarization. On the basis of the CV curves, the diffusion and surface-capacitive reactions are analyzed by: <i>i</i>&nbsp;=&nbsp;a<i>v</i><sup>b</sup> or log(<i>i</i>) = blog(<i>v</i>)+log(<i>a</i>), where <i>a</i> is constant, <i>i</i> is the peak current (mA) and <i>v</i> is the controlled scan rate (mV&nbsp;s<sup>−1</sup>). Here, the b value is used to determine the kinetic process of Na-storage in the electrode. When the b is close to 1, it indicates a predominantly capacitive effect in the charging/discharging process. If the b is 0.5, the charging/discharging process is mainly controlled by diffusion. While b is between 0.5 and 1, a pseudo-capacitive behavior is dominant.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#smll202302706-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="smll202302706-bib-0036_R_d258195871e1578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As seen in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11b,c</a> (Supporting Information), the both calculated b values for reduction and oxidation peaks are between 0.72 and 0.96, which indicates combined surface-capacitive and diffusion-control processes.</p>
         
         <p>In order to calculate the Na<sup>+</sup> ion diffusion coefficient, the relationship of the peak current <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> versus the square root of scan rate <i>v</i> was plotted by using the Randles–Sevcik equation for diffusion-controlled electrochemical reactions (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11d</a>, Supporting Information): <i>i</i><sub>p</sub>&nbsp;=&nbsp;(2.69&nbsp;×&nbsp;10<sup>5</sup>)AD<sup>1/2</sup>C<i>n</i><sup>3/2</sup><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup>, where <i>i</i><sub>p</sub> is the peak current (mA), <i>A</i> is the area of the electrode (1.1314 cm<sup>2</sup>), <i>C</i> is the volume concentration of Na<sup>+</sup> ions in the electrode (0.0014&nbsp;mol cm<sup>–3</sup>), <i>n</i> is the number of electrons transferred in the electrochemical reaction, <i>v</i> is the scan rate (mV s<sup>–1</sup>) and <i>D</i> is the diffusion coefficient of sodium ions in the anode (cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>–1</sup>). The diffusion coefficient (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information) can be obtained from Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11d</a> (Supporting Information) and above equations. On the basis of equations <i>i = k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v + k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v<sup>1/2</sup></i> and <i>i/v<sup>1/2</sup> = k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v<sup>1/2</sup> + k</i><sub>2</sub>, where the values of <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> are constants and <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> denotes the capacitive effect, two groups of peak currents at different sweep speeds are taken respectively to obtain the <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub>. Then the capacitive contribution is <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>/(<i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i>&nbsp;+&nbsp;<i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>0.5</sup>). We found that the dominant capacitive contribution was increased from 85.5% to 95.0% when the scanning rate was increased from 0.1 to 1.0&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11e</a>, Supporting Information), which is much higher than the CoS@C and Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). It is known that high pseudo-capacitance contribution is beneficial for better rate and long-cycling performance of the electrode.</p>
         
         <p>The internal impedance of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC, Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C and CoS@C during charging and discharging was investigated by galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) at 0.2 A g<sup>–1</sup> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The impedance was obtained by: Δ<i>R</i><sub>internal</sub> = Δ<i>U</i><sub>overpotential</sub>/<i>I</i><sub>appiled</sub> <i>m</i>. Where Δ<i>U</i><sub>overpotential</sub> is the voltage difference between the quasi-open circuit voltage-(<i>V</i><sub>QOCV</sub>) and the closed circuit-voltage (<i>V</i><sub>CCV</sub>). <i>I</i><sub>applied</sub> is the applied current; and <i>m</i> is the mass of active material on the electrode. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0005">5c,d</a>, CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC exhibited lower interfacial resistance compared to Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C and CoS@C during both the Na<sup>+</sup> intercalation (discharge)/deintercalation (charge). First-principle calculations were conducted to study the improved electron transfer. A vacuum layer of 15 Å along <i>z</i> direction was inserted to suppress periodic effect. The band gap of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S was calculated by the adsorption of copper atoms on the surface of CoS, and the structural models are displayed in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a> (Supporting Information). As can be seen from Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0005">5e</a>, the conduction band and valence band of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S were almost connected, and the band gap of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S was as low as 0.0044&nbsp;eV. Under the excitation of external energy, electrons could easily transition from valence band to conduction band, which further confirmed its great conductivity. In addition, Figure&nbsp;<a href="#smll202302706-fig-0005">5f</a> shows the DOS distribution of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S, and there was still a distribution of electron clouds near the Fermi level, indicating that electrons can easily transition in this interval, which was in good agreement with the bandgap structure. The good condition for electron transfer enabled the composite displaying high-rate performance.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302706-sec-0030">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302706-sec-0030-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In conclusion, we designed a long-cycling-life sodium-ion battery by using binary metal sulfide hybrid nanocages as anode. The encapsulated CoS and Cu<sub>2</sub>S combination promotes the sodium ion/electron transport while the conductive carbon shell acts as a layer to protect the active material from loss during charging and discharging processes. The battery with CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC hybrid as anode displayed high capacity retention of 435.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 2.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> and 347.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a higher rate of 10.0 A g<sup>−1</sup> after a ultra-long 2300 cycles. The battery also showed better temperature tolerance. Lower internal reaction resistance of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC in contrast to individual sulfide, narrow band gap and high density of the binary sulfides, ensure the rapid Na<sup>+</sup>/e<sup>−</sup> transport even at high currenrt density. The greatly improved electrochemical performance of the binary metal sulfide hybrid nanocages opens new opportunities in designing long-cycling sodium ion batteries and other energy-storage systems.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="smll202302706-sec-0040">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302706-sec-0040-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0050">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0060">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0070">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302706-sec-0070-title"> Materials and Reagents</h4>
                  
                  <p>Chemicals: Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, urea, ammonium fluoride (NH<sub>4</sub>F), ethanol (AR), thioacetamide (TAA), trimethylol aminomethane (Tris), dopamine hydrochloride, concentrated hydrochloric acid, <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone (AR, NMP), and Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O, Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP, 99.9%) were obtained from Aladdin. All chemicals were used directly without further purification.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0080">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302706-sec-0080-title"> Preparation of Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CuS Precursors</h4>
                  
                  <p>In a typical experiment, a mixture solution with 0.146&nbsp;g of Co(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·6H<sub>2</sub>O, 0.09&nbsp;g of urea, 0.02775&nbsp;g of NH<sub>4</sub>F, and 40&nbsp;mL ethanol was prepared in a 100&nbsp;mL beaker after magnetically stirring by an agitator (VRera 85-2) for 30&nbsp;min. Subsequently, 0.1208&nbsp;g of Cu(NO<sub>3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>·3H<sub>2</sub>O and 0.1878&nbsp;g of thioacetamide were added. The obtained solution was transferred to a 50&nbsp;mL polytetrafluoroethylene reaction autoclave in an oven (DHG-9050A, Shanghai Xiangyi Equipment Co., Ltd) and heated at 140&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. After cooling down to room temperature, the sample was washed several times with deionized water/ethanol and dried at 60&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0090">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302706-sec-0090-title"> Synthesis of CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC Hybrid</h4>
                  
                  <p>First a certain amount of Tris solution was mixed with 50&nbsp;mL deionized water and kept stirring for 10&nbsp;min. Then 200&nbsp;uL concentrated hydrochloric acid (12&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span>) was dropwise added. The pH value was adjusted to 8–9 by mixing dopamine hydrochloride into the solution. 150&nbsp;mg of as-prepared Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CuS precursor and dopamine hydrochloride were added into the solution under sonication for 10&nbsp;min, followed by magnetically stirring for 24&nbsp;h. The obtained intermediate product Co<sub>3</sub>S<sub>4</sub>/CuS@polydopamine (PDA) was centrifuged (TG-16G, Changzhou Hundred Million Ability Experimental Equipment Factory), washed by deionized water and ethanol, and dried at 60°C in an oven (DHG-9050A, Shanghai Xiangyi Equipment Co., Ltd) for 12&nbsp;h. The final sample was received after calcination inside a tubular furnace (OTF-1200X, Hefei Kejing Material Technology Co., Ltd) with a nitrogen flow (100 sccm, 99.9%) at 550&nbsp;°C (heating rate: 3&nbsp;°C&nbsp;min<sup>−1</sup>) for 3&nbsp;h.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0100">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302706-sec-0100-title"> Sample Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The crystal structure, morphology, and chemical composition of the sample were characterized by using X-ray powder diffraction (XRD, Bruker D8, Brock AXS GMBH, Germany), field emission scanning electron microscope (SEM, Hitachi 8100, Hitachi Production Co., Ltd) and transmission electron microscope (TEM, HT–7700, Hitachi Production Co., Ltd), respectively. Lattice structure of the sample was checked by using a high-resolution transmission electron microscope (HRTEM, Jeol JEM–2100F). Energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS, SEM, Hitachi 8100) analyzer was employed to investigate the element distribution. Binding energy of the sample was determined by using X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS, Thermo Fisher Escalab 250Xi). The chemical bands were checked by Raman spectroscopy (Renishaw in Via) with a 532&nbsp;nm laser. Mass ratio of the component was studied by thermogravimetric analysis (TGA, Setaram Labsys Evo SDT Q600) in air under a heating rate of 10 per min. The sample surface area and textural properties were analyzed by Brunauer–Emmett–Teller (BET, Micrometer ASAP 2460).</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0110">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302706-sec-0110-title"> Electrochemical Property and Battery Performance Tests</h4>
                  
                  <p>The anode material slurry was made by mixing 70&nbsp;wt% active material, 20&nbsp;wt% acetylene black (Battery level, Hefei Kejing Material Technology Co., Ltd) and 10&nbsp;wt% polyvinylidene fluoride binder (Hefei Kejing Material Technology Co., Ltd) in organic solvent <i>N</i>-methylpyrrolidone. The received slurry was spread on a copper foil with a thickness of 9&nbsp;µm and dried in a vacuum oven (DZF-6020, Beijing Yassin Experimental Equipment co., Ltd) at 80&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The active material CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC mass loading was controlled at 1.1&nbsp;mg cm<sup>−2</sup>. The sample was then cut into a disc with a diameter of 12.0&nbsp;mm by coin cell slicer, which will be used as anode electrode. The cell with sodium metal as counter electrode was assembled in an argon-filled glove box with ≤0.01&nbsp;ppm H<sub>2</sub>O and ≤0.01&nbsp;ppm O<sub>2</sub> (Super (1220/750/900), Shanghai Mievun Electromechanical Technology co., Ltd). 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaCF<sub>3</sub>SO<sub>3</sub> (Nanjing Mojisi Energy Technology Co. Ltd) dissolved in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether was used as electrolyte. For full cell assembly, the cathode was prepared by mixing Na<sub>3</sub>V<sub>2</sub>(PO<sub>4</sub>)<sub>3</sub> (NVP), Super P and Poly(vinylidenefluoride) in a weight ratio of 8:1:1 and coated on an aluminum foil. Prior to assembly, the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S@C-NC anode was pre-sodiated at 0.1&nbsp;A&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> for four cycles. The electrolyte was 1.0&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> (Nanjing Mojisi Energy Technology Co. Ltd) dissolved in diethylene glycol dimethyl ether.</p>
                  
                  <p>All cells were cycled under different conditions on a Neware battery tester (CT-4008, Shenzhen Neware Technology). Cyclic voltammetry (CV) with different scan rates and voltages between 0.2 and 3.0&nbsp;V, and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) with frequency of 100&nbsp;kHz–100&nbsp;MHz were performed on an electrochemical workstation (CHI-660E, Shanghai Chenhua Instrument Co., Ltd). The time–voltage relationship was obtained by using Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) on a Neware battery tester (CT-4008, Shenzhen Neware Technology). At a current density of 0.2 A g<sup>−1</sup>, the cell was charged/discharged to the pre-set potential in 10&nbsp;min and stood by 2&nbsp;h before moving to the next potential.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302706-sec-0120-title"> First-Principle Calculations</h4>
                  
                  <p>Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP) was employed for the first-principle calculations. Structure optimization was accomplished by the Perdew–Burke–Ernzarhof exchange-correlation function under generalized gradient approximation and project-augmented wave atom potentials, where the Coulomb <i>U</i> and exchange <i>J</i> parameters were considered ( <i>U<sub>eff</sub></i> = <i>U</i> − <i>J</i>). To simulate the CoS/Cu<sub>2</sub>S hybrid composite, a vacuum layer ≈15&nbsp;Å in the <i>z</i> direction was inserted to avoid the periodic effect. The primitive structures of CoS and Cu<sub>2</sub>S were obtained from Material Project. During the optimization process, the cutoff energy of the plane-wave basis was set as 500&nbsp;eV, and the structural optimization process stopped when an energy convergence was lower than 10<sup>−5</sup>&nbsp;eV and the atomic force was &lt;0.01&nbsp;eV&nbsp;Å<sup>−1</sup>. Brillouin zone integration was performed using a 3&nbsp;×&nbsp;3&nbsp;×&nbsp;1 <i>k</i>-point mesh.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="smll202302706-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="smll202302706-sec-0130-title"> Statistical Analysis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The images were processed on a Fireworks software (Adobe, San Jose, CA, USA). The XRD, XPS, HRTEM, and Raman spectra were analyzed on Jade software (Materials Data, Livermore, CA, USA), Peak Fit software (Systat software, Inc, San Jose, CA, USA), Digital Micrography software (Gatan Inc, Pleasanton, CA, USA), and OMNIC (Thermo Fisher Scientific Inc, Waltham, MA, USA), respectively. Results were analyzed on an OriginPro software (Origin Lab, Northampton, MA, USA).</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="smll202302706-sec-0140-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>

We also thank the support from Anhui Normal University-Linli New-Energy Joint Laboratory for Materials Chemistry.</p>
